	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">White stork</h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">

<p>The <b>white stork</b> (<i>Ciconia ciconia</i>) is a large <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">bird</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Stork" title="Stork">stork</a> family, Ciconiidae. Its <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> is mainly white, with black on the bird's wings. Adults have long red legs and long pointed red beaks, and measure on average 100–115&#160;cm (39–45&#160;in) from beak tip to end of tail, with a 155–215&#160;cm (61–85&#160;in) wingspan. The two <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>, which differ slightly in size, breed in Europe (north to <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>), northwestern Africa, <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">southwestern Asia</a> (east to southern <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>) and southern Africa. The white stork is a long-distance <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrant</a>, wintering in Africa from tropical <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> to as far south as <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, or on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. When migrating between Europe and Africa, it avoids crossing the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and detours via the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> in the east or the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> in the west, because the air <a href="/wiki/Thermals" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermals">thermals</a> on which it depends for soaring do not form over water.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivore</a>, the white stork eats a wide range of animal prey, including insects, fish, amphibians, reptiles, small mammals and small birds. It takes most of its food from the ground, among low vegetation, and from shallow water. It is a <a href="/wiki/Monogamous" class="mw-redirect" title="Monogamous">monogamous</a> breeder, but does not <a href="/wiki/Pair_bond" title="Pair bond">pair for life</a>. Both members of the pair build a large stick nest, which may be used for several years. Each year the female can lay one <a href="/wiki/Clutch_(eggs)" title="Clutch (eggs)">clutch</a> of usually four eggs, which hatch <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/asynchronous" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:asynchronous">asynchronously</a> 33–34&#160;days after being laid. Both parents take turns incubating the eggs and both feed the young. The young leave the nest 58–64&#160;days after hatching, and continue to be fed by the parents for a further 7–20&#160;days.
</p><p>The white stork has been rated as <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a> by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN). It benefited from human activities during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> as woodland was cleared, but changes in farming methods and industrialisation saw it decline and disappear from parts of Europe in the 19th and early 20th centuries. Conservation and <a href="/wiki/Reintroduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Reintroduction">reintroduction</a> programs across Europe have resulted in the white stork resuming breeding in the Netherlands, Belgium, Switzerland, Sweden and the United Kingdom. It has few natural predators, but may harbour several types of parasite; the plumage is home to <a href="/wiki/Chewing_louse" class="mw-redirect" title="Chewing louse">chewing lice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feather_mite" title="Feather mite">feather mites</a>, while the large nests maintain a diverse range of <a href="/wiki/Mesostigmata" title="Mesostigmata">mesostigmatic</a> mites. This conspicuous species has given rise to many legends across its range, of which the best-known is the story of babies being brought by storks.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_evolution">Taxonomy and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>English naturalist <a href="/wiki/Francis_Willughby" title="Francis Willughby">Francis Willughby</a> wrote about the white stork in the 17th century, having seen a drawing sent to him by his friend and natural history enthusiast Sir <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Browne" title="Thomas Browne">Thomas Brown</a> of Norwich. He named it <i>Ciconia alba</i>. They noted they were occasional vagrants to England, blown there by storms.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> It was one of the many <a href="/wiki/Aves_in_the_10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="Aves in the 10th edition of Systema Naturae">bird species originally described</a> by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in his landmark 1758 <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition of <i>Systema Naturae</i></a>,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> where it was given the binomial name of <i>Ardea ciconia</i>. It was reclassified to (and was designated the <a href="/wiki/Type_species" title="Type species">type species</a> of) the new <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Ciconia" title="Ciconia">Ciconia</a></i> by French <a href="/wiki/Zoologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoologist">zoologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Mathurin_Jacques_Brisson" title="Mathurin Jacques Brisson">Mathurin Jacques Brisson</a> in 1760.<sup id="cite_ref-Brisson1_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brisson1-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-boles05_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boles05-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Both the genus and <a href="/wiki/Specific_name_(zoology)" title="Specific name (zoology)">specific epithet</a>, <i>cĭcōnia</i>, are the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word for "stork",<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> originally recorded in the works of <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The Latin word survives in most Romance languages (<i>cicogna</i>, <i>cigüeña</i>, <i>cegonha</i> and the like). The word <i>stork</i> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> word <i>storc</i>, and appeared in the 10th-century works the <i>Erfurt Glossary</i>, where the word is equated with <i>Ciconia</i>, and <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfric_of_Eynsham" title="Ælfric of Eynsham">Aelfric's</a> <i>Homilies</i>. The word is related to the <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>storah</i>, "stork", and similar words in many other European languages, all of which are descended from the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> <i>sturko-z</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_alba_119.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ciconia_alba_119.jpg/170px-Ciconia_alba_119.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ciconia_alba_119.jpg/255px-Ciconia_alba_119.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ciconia_alba_119.jpg/340px-Ciconia_alba_119.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4848" data-file-height="6680" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_alba_119.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skeleton</div></div></div>
<p>There are two <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>:
</p>
<ul><li><i>C. c. ciconia</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Subspecies#Nominate_subspecies" title="Subspecies">nominate subspecies</a> described by Linnaeus in 1758, breeds from Europe to northwest Africa and westernmost Asia, and in southern Africa, and winters mainly in Africa south of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahara Desert">Sahara Desert</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> though some birds winter in India.<sup id="cite_ref-HBIP1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBIP1-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>C. c. asiatica</i>, described by Russian naturalist <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Severtzov" title="Nikolai Severtzov">Nikolai Severtzov</a> in 1873, breeds in <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a> and winters from Iran to India. It is slightly larger than the nominate subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RasmussenBSA2_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RasmussenBSA2-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The stork family contains six genera in three broad groups: the open-billed and wood storks (<i><a href="/wiki/Mycteria" title="Mycteria">Mycteria</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Anastomus" class="mw-redirect" title="Anastomus">Anastomus</a></i>), the giant storks (<i><a href="/wiki/Ephippiorhynchus" title="Ephippiorhynchus">Ephippiorhynchus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Jabiru" title="Jabiru">Jabiru</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Leptoptilos" title="Leptoptilos">Leptoptilos</a></i>), and the "typical" storks, <i><a href="/wiki/Ciconia" title="Ciconia">Ciconia</a></i>. The typical storks include the white stork and six other <a href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> species,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992437_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992437-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> which are characterised by straight pointed beaks and mainly black and white plumage.<sup id="cite_ref-kahl_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kahl-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Its closest relatives are the larger, black-billed <a href="/wiki/Oriental_stork" title="Oriental stork">Oriental stork</a> (<i>Ciconia boyciana</i>) of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, which was formerly classified as a subspecies of the white stork,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Maguari_stork" title="Maguari stork">maguari stork</a> (<i>C.&#160;maguari</i>) of South America. Close evolutionary relationships within <i>Ciconia</i> are suggested by behavioural similarities and, biochemically, through analysis of both mitochondrial <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_b" title="Cytochrome b">cytochrome <i>b</i></a> gene sequences and <a href="/wiki/DNA-DNA_hybridization" class="mw-redirect" title="DNA-DNA hybridization">DNA-DNA hybridization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Slikas1997_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slikas1997-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A <i>Ciconia</i> fossil representing the <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Proximal_and_distal" title="Anatomical terms of location">distal</a> end of a right <a href="/wiki/Humerus" title="Humerus">humerus</a> has been recovered from <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> beds of <a href="/wiki/Rusinga_Island" title="Rusinga Island">Rusinga Island</a>, Lake Victoria, Kenya.<sup id="cite_ref-Dykes2008_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dykes2008-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The 24–6 million year old fossil could have originated from either a white stork or a <a href="/wiki/Black_stork" title="Black stork">black stork</a> (<i>C.&#160;nigra</i>), which are species of about the same size with very similar bone structures. The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Miocene" title="Middle Miocene">Middle Miocene</a> beds of <a href="/wiki/Maboko_Island" title="Maboko Island">Maboko Island</a> have yielded further remains.<sup id="cite_ref-Dykes2008_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dykes2008-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Stork.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/White_Stork.jpg/220px-White_Stork.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/White_Stork.jpg/330px-White_Stork.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/White_Stork.jpg/440px-White_Stork.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3455" data-file-height="2303" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Stork.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A juvenile feeding on an insect</div></div></div>
<p>The white stork is a large bird. It has a length of 100–115&#160;cm (39–45&#160;in),<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp19773_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp19773-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and a standing height of 100–125&#160;cm (39–49&#160;in). The wingspan is 155–215&#160;cm (61–85&#160;in) and its weight is 2.3–4.5&#160;kg (5.1–9.9&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Like all storks, it has long legs, a long neck and a long straight pointed <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">beak</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992437_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992437-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The sexes are identical in appearance, except that males are larger than females on average.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> is mainly white with black <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather" title="Flight feather">flight feathers</a> and wing <a href="/wiki/Covert_(feather)" class="mw-redirect" title="Covert (feather)">coverts</a>; the black is caused by the pigment <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-grande_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grande-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The breast feathers are long and shaggy forming a ruff which is used in some courtship displays.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977335_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977335-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">irises</a> are dull brown or grey, and the peri-<a href="/wiki/Orbit_(anatomy)" title="Orbit (anatomy)">orbital</a> skin is black. The adult has a bright red beak and red legs,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> the colouration of which is derived from <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a> in the diet. In parts of Spain, studies have shown that the pigment is based on <a href="/wiki/Astaxanthin" title="Astaxanthin">astaxanthin</a> obtained from an introduced species of crayfish (<i><a href="/wiki/Procambarus_clarkii" title="Procambarus clarkii">Procambarus clarkii</a></i>) and the bright red beak colours show up even in nestlings, in contrast to the duller beaks of young white storks elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_-_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Ciconia_ciconia_-_01.jpg/220px-Ciconia_ciconia_-_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Ciconia_ciconia_-_01.jpg/330px-Ciconia_ciconia_-_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Ciconia_ciconia_-_01.jpg/440px-Ciconia_ciconia_-_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_-_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In flight. White storks fly with their necks outstretched.</div></div></div>
<p>As with other storks, the wings are long and broad enabling the bird to soar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992438_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992438-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In flapping <a href="/wiki/Bird_flight" title="Bird flight">flight</a> its wingbeats are slow and regular. It flies with its neck stretched forward and with its long legs extended well beyond the end of its short tail. It walks at a slow and steady pace with its neck upstretched. In contrast, it often hunches its head between its shoulders when resting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977328_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977328-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moulting#Birds" title="Moulting">Moulting</a> has not been extensively studied, but appears to take place throughout the year, with the primary flight feathers replaced over the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977335_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977335-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_-Vogelpark_Avifauna,_The_Netherlands_-juvenile-8a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Head, neck and upper body of a white stork with a long beak with is reddish at the base fading to black at the tip" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Ciconia_ciconia_-Vogelpark_Avifauna%2C_The_Netherlands_-juvenile-8a.jpg/170px-Ciconia_ciconia_-Vogelpark_Avifauna%2C_The_Netherlands_-juvenile-8a.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Ciconia_ciconia_-Vogelpark_Avifauna%2C_The_Netherlands_-juvenile-8a.jpg/255px-Ciconia_ciconia_-Vogelpark_Avifauna%2C_The_Netherlands_-juvenile-8a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Ciconia_ciconia_-Vogelpark_Avifauna%2C_The_Netherlands_-juvenile-8a.jpg/340px-Ciconia_ciconia_-Vogelpark_Avifauna%2C_The_Netherlands_-juvenile-8a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1012" data-file-height="1240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_-Vogelpark_Avifauna,_The_Netherlands_-juvenile-8a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An older juvenile at <a href="/wiki/Vogelpark_Avifauna" title="Vogelpark Avifauna">Vogelpark Avifauna</a>, Netherlands. Beaks turn red starting at the base.</div></div></div>
<p>Upon hatching, the young white stork is partly covered with short, sparse, whitish <a href="/wiki/Down_feather" title="Down feather">down feathers</a>. This early down is replaced about a week later with a denser coat of woolly white down. By three weeks, the young bird acquires black scapulars and <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather" title="Flight feather">flight feathers</a>. On hatching the chick has pinkish legs, which turn to greyish-black as it ages. Its beak is black with a brownish tip.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977335_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977335-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> By the time it <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledges</a>, the juvenile bird's plumage is similar to that of the adult, though its black feathers are often tinged with brown, and its beak and legs are a duller brownish-red or orange. The beak is typically orange or red with a darker tip.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977328_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977328-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The bills gain the adults' red colour the following summer, although the black tips persist in some individuals. Young storks adopt adult plumage by their second summer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_den_Bossche200211_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_den_Bossche200211-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Similar_species">Similar species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Similar species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Within its range the white stork is distinctive when seen on the ground. The winter range of <i>C. c. asiatica</i> overlaps that of the <a href="/wiki/Asian_openbill" title="Asian openbill">Asian openbill</a>, which has similar plumage but a different bill shape. When seen at a distance in flight, the white stork can be confused with several other species with similar underwing patterns, such as the <a href="/wiki/Yellow-billed_stork" title="Yellow-billed stork">yellow-billed stork</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_white_pelican" title="Great white pelican">great white pelican</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_vulture" title="Egyptian vulture">Egyptian vulture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977328_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977328-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The yellow-billed stork is identified by its black tail and a longer, slightly curved, yellow beak. The white stork also tends to be larger than the yellow-billed stork.<sup id="cite_ref-SABirds_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SABirds-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The great white pelican has short legs which do not extend beyond its tail, and it flies with its neck retracted, keeping its head near to its stocky body, giving it a different flight profile.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977228_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977228-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Pelicans also behave differently, soaring in orderly, synchronised flocks rather than in disorganised groups of individuals as the white stork does.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvensson_&amp;_Grant199923_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvensson_&amp;_Grant199923-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The Egyptian vulture is much smaller, with a long wedge-shaped tail, shorter legs and a small yellow-tinged head on a short neck.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvensson_&amp;_Grant199974_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvensson_&amp;_Grant199974-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Common_crane" title="Common crane">common crane</a>, which can also look black and white in strong light, shows longer legs and a longer neck in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvensson_&amp;_Grant199934_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvensson_&amp;_Grant199934-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stork_1000798.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Several black and white birds with long red legs and long red beaks walk in a green grassy area." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Stork_1000798.JPG/220px-Stork_1000798.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Stork_1000798.JPG/330px-Stork_1000798.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Stork_1000798.JPG/440px-Stork_1000798.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stork_1000798.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A flock foraging in Turkey. White storks avoid areas overgrown with tall grass and shrub.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nominate_race" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominate race">nominate race</a> of the white stork has a wide although disjunct summer range across Europe, clustered in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> and North Africa in the west, and much of eastern and central Europe, with 25% of the world's population concentrated in Poland,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> as well as parts of <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">western Asia</a>. The <i>asiatica</i> population of about 1450 birds is restricted to a region in central Asia between the <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a> in western China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_den_Bossche200210_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_den_Bossche200210-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The Xinjiang population is believed to have become extinct around 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">Migration</a> routes extend the range of this species into many parts of Africa and India. Some populations adhere to the eastern migration route, which passes across Israel into eastern and central Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Berthold_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berthold-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-shamounbaranes03_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shamounbaranes03-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A few records of breeding from South Africa have been known since 1933 at <a href="/wiki/Calitzdorp" title="Calitzdorp">Calitzdorp</a>, and about 10 birds have been known to breed since the 1990s around <a href="/wiki/Bredasdorp" title="Bredasdorp">Bredasdorp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RSA_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSA-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> A small population of white storks winters in India and is thought to derive principally from the <i>C.&#160;c.&#160;asiatica</i> population<sup id="cite_ref-RasmussenBSA2_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RasmussenBSA2-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> as flocks of up to 200 birds have been observed on spring migration in the early 1900s through the <a href="/wiki/Kurram_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurram Valley">Kurram Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> However, birds <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">ringed</a> in Germany have been recovered in western (<a href="/wiki/Bikaner" title="Bikaner">Bikaner</a>) and southern (<a href="/wiki/Tirunelveli_district" title="Tirunelveli district">Tirunelveli</a>) India.<sup id="cite_ref-HBIP1_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBIP1-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> An atypical specimen with red orbital skin, a feature of the Oriental white stork, has been recorded<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and further study of the Indian population is required.<sup id="cite_ref-RasmussenBSA2_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RasmussenBSA2-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> North of the breeding range, it is a passage migrant or <a href="/wiki/Vagrancy_(biology)" title="Vagrancy (biology)">vagrant</a> in Finland, Iceland, Ireland, Norway and Sweden, and west to the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a>. In recent years, the range has expanded into western Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sixthcensus_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sixthcensus-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The white stork's preferred feeding grounds are grassy meadows, farmland and shallow wetlands. It avoids areas overgrown with tall grass and shrubs.<sup id="cite_ref-Johst_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johst-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chernobyl (city)">Chernobyl</a> area of northern Ukraine, white stork populations declined after the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">1986 nuclear accident</a> there as farmland was <a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession" title="Ecological succession">succeeded</a> by tall grass shrubs.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In parts of Poland, poor natural foraging grounds have forced birds to seek food at <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">rubbish dumps</a> since 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> White storks have also been reported foraging in rubbish dumps in the Middle East, North Africa and South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The white stork breeds in greater numbers in areas with open grasslands, particularly grassy areas which are wet or periodically flooded, and less in areas with taller vegetation cover such as forest and shrubland.<sup id="cite_ref-Carrascal1993_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrascal1993-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> They make use of grasslands, wetlands, and farmland on the wintering grounds in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-RSA_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSA-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> White storks were probably aided by human activities during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> as woodland was cleared and new pastures and farmland were created, and they were found across much of Europe, breeding as far north as Sweden. The population in Sweden is thought to have established in the 16th century after forests were cut down for agriculture. About 5000 pairs were estimated to breed in the 18th century which declined subsequently. The first accurate census in 1917 found 25 pairs and the last pair failed to breed around 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The white stork has been a rare visitor to the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>, with about 20 birds seen in Britain every year, and prior to 2020 there were no records of nesting since a pair nested atop <a href="/wiki/St_Giles%27_Cathedral" title="St Giles&#39; Cathedral">St Giles High Kirk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, Scotland in 1416.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In 2020, a pair bred in the United Kingdom for the first time in over 600 years,<sup id="cite_ref-CNN2020_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN2020-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> as part of an re-introduction initiative called the White Stork Project.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A decline in population began in the 19th century due to <a href="/wiki/Industrialisation" title="Industrialisation">industrialisation</a> and changes in agricultural methods. White storks no longer nest in many countries, and the current strongholds of the western population are in Portugal, Spain, Ukraine and Poland. In the Iberian Peninsula, populations are concentrated in the southwest, and have also declined due to agricultural practices.<sup id="cite_ref-Carrascal1993_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrascal1993-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> A study published in 2005 found that the <a href="/wiki/Podhale" title="Podhale">Podhale</a> region in the uplands of southern Poland had seen an influx of white storks, which first bred there in 1931 and have nested at progressively higher altitudes since, reaching 890&#160;m (3000&#160;ft) in 1999. The authors proposed that this was related to <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">climate warming</a> and the influx of other animals and plants to higher altitudes.<sup id="cite_ref-Tryjanowski2005_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tryjanowski2005-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> White storks arriving in Poznań province (<a href="/wiki/Greater_Poland_Voivodeship" title="Greater Poland Voivodeship">Greater Poland Voivodeship</a>) in western Poland in spring to breed did so some 10 days earlier in the last twenty years of the 20th century than at the end of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rostocker_Pfeilstorch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rostocker_Pfeilstorch.jpg/220px-Rostocker_Pfeilstorch.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rostocker_Pfeilstorch.jpg/330px-Rostocker_Pfeilstorch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rostocker_Pfeilstorch.jpg/440px-Rostocker_Pfeilstorch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2912" data-file-height="4368" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rostocker_Pfeilstorch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1822, the <i>Rostocker <a href="/wiki/Pfeilstorch" title="Pfeilstorch">Pfeilstorch</a></i> provided early evidence of long-distance stork migration</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Whitestorkflock.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A blue sky with many tiny silhouettes of distant flying birds" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Whitestorkflock.jpg/220px-Whitestorkflock.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Whitestorkflock.jpg/330px-Whitestorkflock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Whitestorkflock.jpg/440px-Whitestorkflock.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="667" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Whitestorkflock.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A flock in migration over Israel. Migrating white storks use the uplift of air <a href="/wiki/Thermal" title="Thermal">thermals</a> to reduce the effort of long-distance flying.</div></div></div>
<p>Systematic research into migration of the white stork began with <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> ornithologist <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Thienemann" title="Johannes Thienemann">Johannes Thienemann</a> who commenced <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">bird ringing</a> studies in 1906 at the <a href="/wiki/Rossitten_Bird_Observatory" title="Rossitten Bird Observatory">Rossitten Bird Observatory</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Curonian_Spit" title="Curonian Spit">Curonian Spit</a> in what was then <a href="/wiki/East_Prussia" title="East Prussia">East Prussia</a>. Although not many storks passed through Rossitten itself, the observatory coordinated the large-scale ringing of the species throughout Germany and elsewhere in Europe. Between 1906 and the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> about 100,000, mainly juvenile, white storks were ringed, with over 2,000 long-distance recoveries of birds wearing Rossitten rings reported between 1908 and 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Routes">Routes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Routes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>White storks fly south from their summer breeding grounds in Europe in August and September, heading for Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Johst_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johst-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> There, they spend the winter in <a href="/wiki/Savannah" class="mw-redirect" title="Savannah">savannah</a> from Kenya and Uganda south to the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Province" title="Cape Province">Cape Province</a> of South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977331_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977331-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In these areas they congregate in large flocks which may exceed a thousand individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977328_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977328-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Some diverge westwards into western Sudan and Chad, and may reach Nigeria.<sup id="cite_ref-Berthold_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berthold-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In spring, the birds return north; they are recorded from Sudan and Egypt from February to April.<sup id="cite_ref-Reed69_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reed69-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> They arrive back in Europe around late March and April,<sup id="cite_ref-Johst_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johst-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> after an average journey of 49 days. By comparison, the autumn journey is completed in about 26 days. <a href="/wiki/Tailwind" class="mw-redirect" title="Tailwind">Tailwinds</a> and scarcity of food and water en route (birds fly faster over regions lacking resources) increase average speed.<sup id="cite_ref-shamounbaranes03_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shamounbaranes03-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2017-03-11_Migratory_storks_resting_in_Rahat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/2017-03-11_Migratory_storks_resting_in_Rahat.jpg/220px-2017-03-11_Migratory_storks_resting_in_Rahat.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/2017-03-11_Migratory_storks_resting_in_Rahat.jpg/330px-2017-03-11_Migratory_storks_resting_in_Rahat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/2017-03-11_Migratory_storks_resting_in_Rahat.jpg/440px-2017-03-11_Migratory_storks_resting_in_Rahat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5218" data-file-height="2609" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2017-03-11_Migratory_storks_resting_in_Rahat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eastern route: storks resting in <a href="/wiki/Rahat" title="Rahat">Rahat</a> (spring 2017).</div></div></div>
<p>To avoid a long sea crossing over the Mediterranean, birds from central Europe either follow an eastern migration route by crossing the <a href="/wiki/Bosphorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Bosphorus">Bosphorus</a> to Turkey, traversing the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, then bypassing the <a href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahara Desert">Sahara Desert</a> by following the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> valley southwards, or follow a western route over the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> These migration corridors maximise help from the thermals and thus save energy.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The eastern route is by far the more important with 530,000 white storks using it annually, making the species the second commonest migrant there (after the <a href="/wiki/European_honey_buzzard" title="European honey buzzard">European honey buzzard</a>). The flocks of migrating <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">raptors</a>, white storks and <a href="/wiki/Great_white_pelican" title="Great white pelican">great white pelicans</a> can stretch for 200&#160;km (125&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewton201074–75_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewton201074–75-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The eastern route is twice as long as the western, but storks take the same time to reach the wintering grounds by either.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewton2010144_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewton2010144-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Juvenile white storks set off on their first southward migration in an inherited direction but, if displaced from that bearing by weather conditions, they are unable to compensate, and may end up in a new wintering location. Adults can compensate for strong winds and adjust their direction to finish at their normal winter sites, because they are familiar with the location. For the same reason, all spring migrants, even those from displaced wintering locations, can find their way back to the traditional breeding sites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewton2010229_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewton2010229-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> An experiment with young birds raised in captivity in Kaliningrad and released in the absence of wild storks to show them the way revealed that they appeared to have an instinct to fly south, although the scatter in direction was large.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernetsov2004_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernetsov2004-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Energetics">Energetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Energetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Whitestorkflight.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Whitestorkflight.png/220px-Whitestorkflight.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Whitestorkflight.png/330px-Whitestorkflight.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Whitestorkflight.png/440px-Whitestorkflight.png 2x" data-file-width="1248" data-file-height="957" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Whitestorkflight.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Otto_Lilienthal" title="Otto Lilienthal">Otto Lilienthal</a> studied the flight of storks in 1889 while designing his glider. Sketches by his brother <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Lilienthal" title="Gustav Lilienthal">Gustav Lilienthal</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>White storks rely on the uplift of air <a href="/wiki/Thermal" title="Thermal">thermals</a> to soar and glide the long distances of their annual migrations between Europe and <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>. For many, the shortest route would take them over the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>; however, since air thermals do not form over water, they generally detour over land to avoid the trans-Mediterranean flights that would require prolonged energetic wing flapping.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> It has been estimated that flapping flight metabolises 23 times more body fat than soaring flight per distance travelled.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Thus, flocks spiral upwards on rising warm air until they emerge at the top, up to 1,200–1,500&#160;m (3,900–4,900&#160;ft) above the ground (though one record from Western Sudan observed an altitude of 3,300&#160;m (10,800&#160;ft)).<sup id="cite_ref-Reed69_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reed69-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Long flights over water may occasionally be undertaken. A young white stork <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">ringed</a> at the nest in Denmark subsequently appeared in England, where it spent some days before moving on. It was later seen flying over <a href="/wiki/St_Mary%27s,_Isles_of_Scilly" title="St Mary&#39;s, Isles of Scilly">St Mary's, Isles of Scilly</a>, and arrived in a poor condition in <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a> three days later. That island is 500&#160;km (320&#160;mi) from Africa, and twice as far from the European mainland.<sup id="cite_ref-Cocker_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cocker-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Migration through the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> may be hampered by the <i><a href="/wiki/Khamsin" title="Khamsin">khamsin</a></i>, winds bringing gusty overcast days unsuitable for flying. In these situations, flocks of white storks sit out the adverse weather on the ground, standing and facing into the wind.<sup id="cite_ref-Reed69_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reed69-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_-Tsavo_East_National_Park,_Kenya-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Ciconia_ciconia_-Tsavo_East_National_Park%2C_Kenya-8.jpg/170px-Ciconia_ciconia_-Tsavo_East_National_Park%2C_Kenya-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Ciconia_ciconia_-Tsavo_East_National_Park%2C_Kenya-8.jpg/255px-Ciconia_ciconia_-Tsavo_East_National_Park%2C_Kenya-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Ciconia_ciconia_-Tsavo_East_National_Park%2C_Kenya-8.jpg/340px-Ciconia_ciconia_-Tsavo_East_National_Park%2C_Kenya-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1511" data-file-height="2052" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_-Tsavo_East_National_Park,_Kenya-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult in <a href="/wiki/Tsavo_East_National_Park" title="Tsavo East National Park">Tsavo East National Park</a>, Kenya. The lower parts of its legs are a whitish colour due to being covered with its droppings—an example of <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">thermoregulation</a> by <a href="/wiki/Urohidrosis" title="Urohidrosis">urohidrosis</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The white stork is a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gregarious" class="extiw" title="wikt:gregarious">gregarious</a> bird; flocks of thousands of individuals have been recorded on migration routes and at wintering areas in Africa. Non-breeding birds gather in groups of 40 or 50 during the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977328_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977328-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The smaller dark-plumaged <a href="/wiki/Abdim%27s_stork" title="Abdim&#39;s stork">Abdim's stork</a> is often encountered with white stork flocks in southern Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-SABirdssinc_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SABirdssinc-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Breeding_pair" title="Breeding pair">Breeding pairs</a> of white stork may gather in small groups to hunt, and colony nesting has been recorded in some areas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977332_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977332-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> However, groups among white stork colonies vary widely in size and the social structure is loosely defined; young breeding storks are often restricted to peripheral nests, while older storks attain higher breeding success while occupying the better quality nests toward the centres of breeding colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-Vergara2006_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vergara2006-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Social structure and group cohesion is maintained by <a href="/wiki/Altruism" title="Altruism">altruistic</a> behaviours such as <a href="/wiki/Allopreening" class="mw-redirect" title="Allopreening">allopreening</a>. White storks exhibit this behaviour exclusively at the nest site. Standing birds preen the heads of sitting birds, sometimes these are parents grooming juveniles, and sometimes juveniles preen each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrison65_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison65-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Unlike most storks, it never adopts a spread-winged posture, though it is known to droop its wings (holding them away from its body with the <a href="/wiki/Primary_feather" class="mw-redirect" title="Primary feather">primary feathers</a> pointing downwards) when its plumage is wet.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A white stork's droppings, containing faeces and <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a>, are sometimes directed onto its own legs, making them appear white.<sup id="cite_ref-SABirds_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SABirds-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The resulting evaporation provides cooling and is termed <a href="/wiki/Urohidrosis" title="Urohidrosis">urohidrosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Birds that have been <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">ringed</a> can sometimes be affected by the accumulation of droppings around the ring leading to constriction and leg trauma.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The white stork has also been noted for tool use by squeezing moss in the beak to drip water into the mouths of its chicks.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">Communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The adult white stork's main sound is noisy bill-clattering, which has been likened to distant machine gun fire. The bird makes these sounds by rapidly opening and closing its beak so that a knocking sound is made each time its beak closes. The clattering is amplified by its <a href="/wiki/Gular_skin" title="Gular skin">throat pouch</a>, which acts as a <a href="/wiki/Resonator" title="Resonator">resonator</a>. Used in a variety of social interactions, bill-clattering generally grows louder the longer it lasts, and takes on distinctive rhythms depending on the situation—for example, slower during copulation and briefer when given as an <a href="/wiki/Alarm_signal" title="Alarm signal">alarm call</a>. The only vocal sound adult birds generate is a weak barely audible hiss; however, young birds can generate a harsh hiss, various cheeping sounds, and a cat-like mew they use to beg for food. Like the adults, young also clatter their beaks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977334_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977334-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The <i>up-down display</i> is used for a number of interactions with other members of the species. Here a stork quickly throws its head backwards so that its crown rests on its back before slowly bringing its head and neck forwards again, and this is repeated several times. The display is used as a greeting between birds, post coitus, and also as a <a href="/wiki/Threat_display" class="mw-redirect" title="Threat display">threat display</a>. Breeding pairs are <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territorial</a> over the summer, and use this display, as well as crouching forward with the tails cocked and wings extended.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977333_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977333-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding_and_lifespan">Breeding and lifespan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Breeding and lifespan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_-Iglesia_de_San_Isidoro,_Ciguenas_en_el_campanario,_Salamanca,_Spain-18June2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="At least eight tall, black and white birds, in three nests on the roof of a building." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ciconia_ciconia_-Iglesia_de_San_Isidoro%2C_Ciguenas_en_el_campanario%2C_Salamanca%2C_Spain-18June2006.jpg/220px-Ciconia_ciconia_-Iglesia_de_San_Isidoro%2C_Ciguenas_en_el_campanario%2C_Salamanca%2C_Spain-18June2006.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ciconia_ciconia_-Iglesia_de_San_Isidoro%2C_Ciguenas_en_el_campanario%2C_Salamanca%2C_Spain-18June2006.jpg/330px-Ciconia_ciconia_-Iglesia_de_San_Isidoro%2C_Ciguenas_en_el_campanario%2C_Salamanca%2C_Spain-18June2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ciconia_ciconia_-Iglesia_de_San_Isidoro%2C_Ciguenas_en_el_campanario%2C_Salamanca%2C_Spain-18June2006.jpg/440px-Ciconia_ciconia_-Iglesia_de_San_Isidoro%2C_Ciguenas_en_el_campanario%2C_Salamanca%2C_Spain-18June2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1908" data-file-height="1712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_-Iglesia_de_San_Isidoro,_Ciguenas_en_el_campanario,_Salamanca,_Spain-18June2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nests on a belfry in Spain. White storks often form small nesting colonies.</div></div></div>
<p>The white stork breeds in open farmland areas with access to marshy wetlands, building a large stick <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">nest</a> in trees, on buildings, or on purpose-built man-made platforms.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Each nest is 1–2&#160;m (3.3–6.6&#160;ft) in depth, 0.8–1.5&#160;m (2.6–4.9&#160;ft) in diameter, and 60–250&#160;kg (130–550&#160;lb) in weight.<sup id="cite_ref-AZ-2009_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AZ-2009-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Nests are built in loose colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-Vergara2006_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vergara2006-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Not persecuted as it is viewed as a good omen, it often nests close to human habitation; in southern Europe, nests can be seen on churches and other buildings. The nest is typically used year after year especially by older males. The males arrive earlier in the season and choose the nests. Larger nests are associated with greater numbers of young successfully fledged, and appear to be sought after.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Nest change is often related to a change in the pairing and failure to raise young the previous year, and younger birds are more likely to change nesting sites.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Although a pair may be found to occupy a nest, partners may change several times during the early stages and breeding activities begin only after a stable pairing is achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_in_Marrakesh_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ciconia_ciconia_in_Marrakesh_02.jpg/220px-Ciconia_ciconia_in_Marrakesh_02.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ciconia_ciconia_in_Marrakesh_02.jpg/330px-Ciconia_ciconia_in_Marrakesh_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ciconia_ciconia_in_Marrakesh_02.jpg/440px-Ciconia_ciconia_in_Marrakesh_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2385" data-file-height="1633" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_in_Marrakesh_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mating</div></div></div>
<p>Several bird species often nest within the large nests of the white stork. Regular occupants are <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house sparrows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tree_sparrow" class="mw-redirect" title="Tree sparrow">tree sparrows</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Common_starling" title="Common starling">common starlings</a>; less common residents include <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_kestrel" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian kestrel">Eurasian kestrels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owls</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_roller" title="European roller">European rollers</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_wagtail" title="White wagtail">white wagtails</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_redstart" title="Black redstart">black redstarts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_jackdaw" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian jackdaw">Eurasian jackdaws</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_sparrow" title="Spanish sparrow">Spanish sparrows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Paired birds greet by engaging in up-down and head-shaking crouch displays, and clattering the beak while throwing back the head.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Pairs copulate frequently throughout the month before eggs are laid. High-frequency pair copulation is usually associated with <a href="/wiki/Sperm_competition" title="Sperm competition">sperm competition</a> and high frequency of <a href="/wiki/Extra-pair_copulation" title="Extra-pair copulation">extra-pair copulation</a>; however, extra-pair copulation is infrequent in white storks.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A white stork pair raises a single brood a year. The female typically lays four <a href="/wiki/Bird_egg" title="Bird egg">eggs</a>, though <a href="/wiki/Clutch_(eggs)" title="Clutch (eggs)">clutches</a> of one to seven have been recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977334_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977334-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The eggs are white, but often look dirty or yellowish due to a <a href="/wiki/Glutin" class="mw-redirect" title="Glutin">glutinous</a> covering. They typically measure 73&#160;mm ×&#160;52&#160;mm (2.9&#160;in ×&#160;2.0&#160;in),<sup id="cite_ref-Oologia_neerlandica_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oologia_neerlandica-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and weigh 96–129&#160;g (3.4–4.6&#160;oz),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977334_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977334-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> of which about 11&#160;g (0.39&#160;oz) is shell.<sup id="cite_ref-Oologia_neerlandica_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oologia_neerlandica-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Incubation begins as soon as the first egg is laid, so the brood hatches asynchronously, beginning 33 to 34&#160;days later. The first hatchling typically has a competitive edge over the others. While stronger chicks are not aggressive towards weaker siblings, as is the case in some species, weak or small chicks are sometimes killed by their parents.<sup id="cite_ref-Zielinski_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zielinski-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> This behaviour occurs in times of food shortage to reduce brood size and hence increase the chance of survival of the remaining nestlings. White stork nestlings do not attack each other, and their parents' method of feeding them (disgorging large amounts of food at once) means that stronger siblings cannot outcompete weaker ones for food directly, hence parental infanticide is an efficient way of reducing brood size. Despite this, this behaviour has not commonly been observed.<sup id="cite_ref-Zielinski_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zielinski-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cigogne_blanche_MHNT.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cigogne_blanche_MHNT.jpg/170px-Cigogne_blanche_MHNT.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cigogne_blanche_MHNT.jpg/255px-Cigogne_blanche_MHNT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Cigogne_blanche_MHNT.jpg/340px-Cigogne_blanche_MHNT.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5111" data-file-height="4329" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cigogne_blanche_MHNT.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egg</div></div></div>
<p>The temperature and weather around the time of hatching in spring is important; cool temperatures and wet weather increase chick mortality and reduce breeding success rates.<sup id="cite_ref-Carrascal1993_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrascal1993-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Somewhat unexpectedly, studies have found that later-hatching chicks which successfully reach adulthood produce more chicks than do their earlier-hatching nestmates.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The body weight of the chicks increases rapidly in the first few weeks and reaches a plateau of about 3.4&#160;kg (7.5&#160;lb) in 45&#160;days. The length of the beak increases linearly for about 50&#160;days.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Young birds are fed with earthworms and insects, which are regurgitated by the parents onto the floor of the nest. Older chicks reach into the mouths of parents to obtain food.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Chicks fledge 58 to 64&#160;days after hatching.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_den_Bossche20028_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_den_Bossche20028-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>White storks generally begin breeding when about four years old, although the age of first breeding has been recorded as early as two years and as late as seven years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977335_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977335-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The oldest known wild white stork lived for 39 years after being <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">ringed</a> in Switzerland,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> while captive birds have lived for more than 35 years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>White storks consume a wide variety of animal prey. They prefer to forage in meadows that are within roughly 5&#160;km (3&#160;mi) of their nest and sites where the vegetation is shorter so that their prey is more accessible.<sup id="cite_ref-Johst_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johst-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Their diet varies according to season, locality and prey availability. Common food items include insects (primarily beetles, grasshoppers, locusts and crickets), earthworms, reptiles, amphibians, particularly frog species such as the <a href="/wiki/Edible_frog" title="Edible frog">edible frog</a> (<i>Pelophylax</i> kl. <i>esculentus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Common_frog" title="Common frog">common frog</a> (<i>Rana temporaria</i>) and small mammals such as voles, moles and shrews. Less commonly, they also eat bird eggs and young birds, fish, molluscs, crustaceans and scorpions. They hunt mainly during the day, swallowing small prey whole, but killing and breaking apart larger prey before swallowing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECramp1977332_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECramp1977332-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Rubber bands are mistaken for earthworms and consumed, occasionally resulting in fatal blockage of the digestive tract.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stork_picking_at_rabbit.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stork_picking_at_rabbit.jpg/220px-Stork_picking_at_rabbit.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stork_picking_at_rabbit.jpg/330px-Stork_picking_at_rabbit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Stork_picking_at_rabbit.jpg/440px-Stork_picking_at_rabbit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3637" data-file-height="2881" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stork_picking_at_rabbit.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>White stork picking a dead young rabbit</div></div></div>
<p>Birds returning to Latvia during spring have been shown to locate their prey, moor frogs (<i><a href="/wiki/Rana_arvalis" class="mw-redirect" title="Rana arvalis">Rana arvalis</a></i>), by homing in on the mating calls produced by aggregations of male frogs.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The diet of non-breeding birds is similar to that of breeding birds, but food items are more often taken from dry areas.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> White storks wintering in western India have been observed to follow <a href="/wiki/Blackbuck" title="Blackbuck">blackbuck</a> to capture insects disturbed by them.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Wintering white storks in India sometimes forage along with the <a href="/wiki/Woolly-necked_stork" title="Woolly-necked stork">woolly-necked stork</a> (<i>Ciconia episcopus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Pande_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Food piracy has been recorded in India with a rodent captured by a <a href="/wiki/Western_marsh_harrier" title="Western marsh harrier">western marsh harrier</a> appropriated by a white stork, while <a href="/wiki/Montagu%27s_harrier" title="Montagu&#39;s harrier">Montagu's harrier</a> is known to harass white storks foraging for voles in some parts of Poland.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> White storks can exploit landfill sites for food during the breeding season, migration period and winter.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasites_and_diseases">Parasites and diseases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Parasites and diseases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>White stork nests are <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a> for an array of small arthropods, particularly over the warmer months after the birds arrive to breed. Nesting over successive years, the storks bring more material to line their nests and layers of organic material accumulate within them. Not only do their bodies tend to regulate temperatures within the nest, but excrement, food remains and feather and skin fragments provide nourishment for a large and diverse population of free-living <a href="/wiki/Mesostigmata" title="Mesostigmata">mesostigmatic</a> mites. A survey of twelve nests found 13,352 individuals of 34 species, the most common being <i><a href="/wiki/Macrocheles_merdarius" title="Macrocheles merdarius">Macrocheles merdarius</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Macrocheles_robustulus" title="Macrocheles robustulus">M.&#160;robustulus</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uroobovella_pyriformis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Uroobovella pyriformis (page does not exist)">Uroobovella pyriformis</a></i> and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trichouropoda_orbicularis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Trichouropoda orbicularis (page does not exist)">Trichouropoda orbicularis</a></i>, which together represented almost 85% of all the specimens collected. These feed on the eggs and larvae of insects and on <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>, which are abundant in the nest litter. These mites are dispersed by <a href="/wiki/Coprophagia" title="Coprophagia">coprophilous</a> beetles, often of the family <a href="/wiki/Scarabaeidae" title="Scarabaeidae">Scarabaeidae</a>, or on dung brought by the storks during nest construction. Parasitic mites do not occur, perhaps being controlled by the predatory species. The overall effect of the mite population is unclear, the mites may have a role in suppressing harmful organisms (and hence be beneficial), or they may themselves have an adverse effect on nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-bloszyk2005_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloszyk2005-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bajerlein_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bajerlein-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The birds themselves host species belonging to more than four genera of <a href="/wiki/Feather_mite" title="Feather mite">feather mites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> These mites, including <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Freyanopterolichus_pelargicus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Freyanopterolichus pelargicus (page does not exist)">Freyanopterolichus pelargicus</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-TrouessartMegnin1885_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TrouessartMegnin1885-105">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MuseumCentralAfrica_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MuseumCentralAfrica-106">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelargolichus_didactylus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pelargolichus didactylus (page does not exist)">Pelargolichus didactylus</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-MuseumCentralAfrica_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MuseumCentralAfrica-106">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> live on fungi growing on the feathers. The fungi found on the plumage may feed on the <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratin</a> of the outer feathers or on feather oil.<sup id="cite_ref-fungi_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fungi-107">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chewing_lice" class="mw-redirect" title="Chewing lice">Chewing lice</a> such as <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Colpocephalum_zebra&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Colpocephalum zebra (page does not exist)">Colpocephalum zebra</a></i> tend to be found on the wings, and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neophilopterus_incompletus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Neophilopterus incompletus (page does not exist)">Neophilopterus incompletus</a></i> elsewhere on the body.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The white stork also carries several types of internal parasites, including <i><a href="/wiki/Toxoplasma_gondii" title="Toxoplasma gondii">Toxoplasma gondii</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Intestinal_parasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Intestinal parasite">intestinal parasites</a> of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Giardia" title="Giardia">Giardia</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> A study of 120 white stork carcasses from <a href="/wiki/Saxony-Anhalt" title="Saxony-Anhalt">Saxony-Anhalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brandenburg" title="Brandenburg">Brandenburg</a> in Germany yielded eight species of <a href="/wiki/Trematoda" title="Trematoda">trematode</a> (fluke), four <a href="/wiki/Cestoda" title="Cestoda">cestode</a> (tapeworm) species, and at least three species of <a href="/wiki/Nematoda" class="mw-redirect" title="Nematoda">nematode</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> One species of fluke, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chaunocephalus_ferox&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Chaunocephalus ferox (page does not exist)">Chaunocephalus ferox</a></i>, caused lesions in the wall of the small intestine in a number of birds admitted to two rehabilitation centres in central Spain, and was associated with reduced weight. It is a recognised pathogen and cause of morbidity in the <a href="/wiki/Asian_openbill" title="Asian openbill">Asian openbill</a> (<i>Anastomus oscitans</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> More recently, the thorough study performed by J. Sitko and P. Heneberg in the Czech Republic in 1962–2013 suggested that the central European white storks host 11 helminth species. <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chaunocephalus_ferox&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Chaunocephalus ferox (page does not exist)">Chaunocephalus ferox</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tylodelphys_excavata&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tylodelphys excavata (page does not exist)">Tylodelphys excavata</a></i> and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dictymetra_discoidea&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dictymetra discoidea (page does not exist)">Dictymetra discoidea</a></i> were reported to be the dominant ones. The other species found included <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cathaemasia_hians&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cathaemasia hians (page does not exist)">Cathaemasia hians</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinochasmus_spinulosus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Echinochasmus spinulosus (page does not exist)">Echinochasmus spinulosus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Echinostoma_revolutum" title="Echinostoma revolutum">Echinostoma revolutum</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinostoma_sudanense&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Echinostoma sudanense (page does not exist)">Echinostoma sudanense</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Duboisia_syriaca&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Duboisia syriaca (page does not exist)">Duboisia syriaca</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Apharyngostrigea_cornu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Apharyngostrigea cornu (page does not exist)">Apharyngostrigea cornu</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capillaria_sp.&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Capillaria sp. (page does not exist)">Capillaria sp.</a></i> and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dictymetra_discoidea&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dictymetra discoidea (page does not exist)">Dictymetra discoidea</a></i>. Juvenile white storks were shown to host less species, but the intensity of infection was higher in the juveniles than in the adult storks.<sup id="cite_ref-Sitko_&amp;_Heneberg_2015_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sitko_&amp;_Heneberg_2015-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/West_Nile_virus" title="West Nile virus">West Nile virus</a> (WNV) is mainly a bird infection that is transmitted between birds by <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquitos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Migrating birds appear to be important in spread of the virus,<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> the ecology of which remains poorly known.<sup id="cite_ref-Zeller2004_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zeller2004-116">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> On 26 August 1998, a flock of about 1,200 migrating white storks that had been blown off course on their southward journey landed in <a href="/wiki/Eilat" title="Eilat">Eilat</a>, in southern Israel. The flock was stressed as it had resorted to flapping flight to return to its migratory route, and a number of birds died. A virulent strain of West Nile virus was isolated from the brains of eleven dead juveniles. Other white storks subsequently tested in Israel have shown anti-WNV <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In 2008 three juvenile white storks from a Polish wildlife refuge yielded <a href="/wiki/Serostatus" title="Serostatus">seropositive</a> results indicating exposure to the virus, but the context or existence of the virus in Poland is unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
