<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_stork.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Europe_stork.png/220px-Europe_stork.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Europe_stork.png/330px-Europe_stork.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Europe_stork.png/440px-Europe_stork.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_stork.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Results of the 2004/05 white stork census in Europe (numbers of breeding pairs)</div></div></div><p>The white stork's decline due to <a href="/wiki/Industrialisation" title="Industrialisation">industrialisation</a> and agricultural changes (principally the draining of wetlands and conversion of meadows to crops such as <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>) began in the 19th century: the last wild individual in Belgium was seen in 1895, in Sweden in 1955, in Switzerland in 1950 and in the Netherlands in 1991. However, the species has since been reintroduced to many regions.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> It has been rated as <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a> by the IUCN since 1994, after being evaluated as <a href="/wiki/Near_threatened" class="mw-redirect" title="Near threatened">near threatened</a> in 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The white stork is one of the species to which the <i>Agreement on the Conservation of African-Eurasian Migratory Waterbirds</i> (<a href="/wiki/AEWA" class="mw-redirect" title="AEWA">AEWA</a>) applies.<sup id="cite_ref-AEWA_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEWA-120">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Parties to the agreement are required to engage in a wide range of conservation strategies described in a detailed action plan. The plan is intended to address key issues such as species and habitat conservation, management of human activities, research, education, and implementation.<sup id="cite_ref-AEWAintro_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEWAintro-121">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Threats include the continued loss of wetlands, collisions with overhead power lines, use of <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">persistent pesticides</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a>) to combat <a href="/wiki/Desert_locust" title="Desert locust">locusts</a> in Africa, and largely illegal hunting on migration routes and wintering grounds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_juv_small.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Three long-legged, long-billed black and white birds stand on a huge pile of sticks atop an artificial platform on a pole" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Ciconia_ciconia_juv_small.jpg/220px-Ciconia_ciconia_juv_small.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Ciconia_ciconia_juv_small.jpg/330px-Ciconia_ciconia_juv_small.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Ciconia_ciconia_juv_small.jpg/440px-Ciconia_ciconia_juv_small.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="647" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciconia_ciconia_juv_small.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A man-made nest platform in Poland built as a conservation measure and to prevent storks disrupting electricity supplies through nesting on pylons. Three young white storks are on the top of the nest and two <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_tree_sparrow" title="Eurasian tree sparrow">Eurasian tree sparrows</a> are perching on the side of the nest.</div></div></div>
<p>A large population of white storks breeds in central (Poland, Ukraine and Germany) and southern Europe (Spain and Turkey). In a 2004/05 census, there were 52,500 pairs in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, 30,000 pairs in <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, 20,000 pairs in <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, 13,000 pairs in <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> (the highest known density of this species in the world), 10,700 pairs in <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>, and 10,200 in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. There were around 5,500 pairs in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, 5,300 in <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> and an estimated 4,956 breeding pairs in <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nabu_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nabu-122">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, the majority of the total 4,482 pairs were in the eastern region, especially in the states of <a href="/wiki/Brandenburg" title="Brandenburg">Brandenburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mecklenburg-Vorpommern" title="Mecklenburg-Vorpommern">Mecklenburg-Vorpommern</a> (1296 and 863 pairs in 2008 respectively).<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Apart from <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> (33,217 and 7,684 pairs in 2004/05 respectively), populations are generally much less stable. The Danish population declined to just three pairs in 2005. In the eastern Mediterranean region <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> has a sizeable population of 6,195 pairs, and <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> 2,139 pairs. In Western Europe the white stork remains a rare bird despite conservation efforts. In 2004 <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> had only 973 pairs, and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> 528 pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-nabu_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nabu-122">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In Armenia the population of the white stork slightly increased in the period between 2005 and 2015, and by last data reached 652 pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1980s, the population had fallen to fewer than nine pairs in the entire upper <a href="/wiki/Rhine_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhine River">Rhine River</a> valley, an area closely identified with the white stork for centuries. Conservation efforts successfully increased the population of birds there to 270 pairs (in 2008), largely due to the actions of the Association for the Protection and Reintroduction of Storks in <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lorraine_(region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lorraine (region)">Lorraine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The reintroduction of <a href="/wiki/Zoo" title="Zoo">zoo</a>-reared birds has halted further declines in Italy, the Netherlands, and Switzerland. There were 601 pairs breeding in <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and around 700 pairs in the Netherlands in 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-Vogelbescherming_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vogelbescherming-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> and few pairs also breed in South Africa, typically recent colonists from within the normal wintering population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In Poland, electric poles have been modified with a platform at the top to prevent the white stork's large nest from disrupting the electricity supply, and sometimes nests are moved from an electric pole to a man-made platform.<sup id="cite_ref-AZ-2009_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AZ-2009-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Introductions of zoo-reared birds in the Netherlands has been followed up by feeding and nest-building programs by volunteers.<sup id="cite_ref-Vogelbescherming_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vogelbescherming-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Similar reintroduction programs are taking place in Sweden,<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> and Switzerland,<sup id="cite_ref-Moritzi_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moritzi-130">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> where 175 pairs were recorded breeding in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Long-term viability of the population in Switzerland is unclear as breeding success rates are low, and supplementary feeding does not appear to be of benefit.<sup id="cite_ref-Moritzi_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moritzi-130">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> However, as of 2017, 470 adults and 757 young ones were recorded in Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2019, 24 juveniles were released at the Knepp Estate in <a href="/wiki/West_Sussex" title="West Sussex">West Sussex</a>, and others at a site near <a href="/wiki/Tunbridge_Wells" class="mw-redirect" title="Tunbridge Wells">Tunbridge Wells</a> and at the Wintershall Estate, near <a href="/wiki/Godalming" title="Godalming">Godalming</a>, as part of a project to reintroduce the white stork as a breeding species in <a href="/wiki/South_East_England" title="South East England">South East England</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-BG-2019_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BG-2019-133">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> for the first time since 1416.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> In 2020, the program was successful with the birth of five baby storks.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN2020_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN2020-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_associations">Cultural associations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cultural associations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:J%C3%B3zef_Che%C5%82mo%C5%84ski_-_Bociany_-_01_(MP_561_MNW).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/J%C3%B3zef_Che%C5%82mo%C5%84ski_-_Bociany_-_01_%28MP_561_MNW%29.jpg/220px-J%C3%B3zef_Che%C5%82mo%C5%84ski_-_Bociany_-_01_%28MP_561_MNW%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/J%C3%B3zef_Che%C5%82mo%C5%84ski_-_Bociany_-_01_%28MP_561_MNW%29.jpg/330px-J%C3%B3zef_Che%C5%82mo%C5%84ski_-_Bociany_-_01_%28MP_561_MNW%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/J%C3%B3zef_Che%C5%82mo%C5%84ski_-_Bociany_-_01_%28MP_561_MNW%29.jpg/440px-J%C3%B3zef_Che%C5%82mo%C5%84ski_-_Bociany_-_01_%28MP_561_MNW%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2297" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:J%C3%B3zef_Che%C5%82mo%C5%84ski_-_Bociany_-_01_(MP_561_MNW).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Bociany</i> (<i>Storks</i>), a painting by <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Che%C5%82mo%C5%84ski" title="Józef Chełmoński">Józef Chełmoński</a> (1849–1914)</div></div></div>
<p>Due to its large size, predation on vermin, and nesting behaviour close to human settlements and on rooftops, the white stork has an imposing presence that has influenced human culture and folklore.<sup id="cite_ref-Cocker_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cocker-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hebrew" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> word for the white stork is <i>chasidah</i> (חסידה), meaning "merciful" or "kind".<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman mythology</a> portray storks as models of parental devotion. The 3rd century Roman writer <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Aelianus" title="Claudius Aelianus">Aelian</a> citing the authority of Alexander of Myndus noted in his <i>De natura animalium</i> (book 3, chapter 23) that aged storks flew away to oceanic islands where they were transformed into humans as a reward for their piety towards their parents.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> The bird is featured in at least three of <a href="/wiki/Aesop%27s_Fables" title="Aesop&#39;s Fables">Aesop's Fables</a>: <a href="/wiki/The_Fox_and_the_Stork" title="The Fox and the Stork">The Fox and the Stork</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Farmer_and_the_Stork" title="The Farmer and the Stork">The Farmer and the Stork</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Frogs_Who_Desired_a_King" title="The Frogs Who Desired a King">The Frogs Who Desired a King</a>. Storks were also thought to care for their aged parents, feeding them and even transporting them, and children's books depicted them as a model of filial values. A Greek law called <i>Pelargonia</i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> word <i>pelargos</i> for stork, required citizens to take care of their aged parents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1_11-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott1992460-1-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The Greeks also held that killing a stork could be punished with death.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Storks were allegedly protected in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Thessaly" title="Ancient Thessaly">Ancient Thessaly</a> as they hunted snakes, and widely held to be <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>'s "white bird".<sup id="cite_ref-Royds14_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Royds14-138">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Roman writers noted the white stork's arrival in spring, which alerted farmers to plant their vines.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Followers of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> revered storks because they made an annual pilgrimage to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> on their migration.<sup id="cite_ref-sax_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sax-140">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Some of the earliest understanding on bird migration were initiated by an interest in white storks; <i><a href="/wiki/Pfeilstorch" title="Pfeilstorch">Pfeilstörche</a></i> ("arrow storks") were found in Europe with African <a href="/wiki/Arrow" title="Arrow">arrows</a> embedded in their bodies. A well-known example of such a stork found in the summer of 1822 in the German town of <a href="/wiki/Kl%C3%BCtz" title="Klütz">Klütz</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecklenburg" title="Mecklenburg">Mecklenburg</a> was made into a mounted taxidermy specimen, complete with the ornate African arrow, that is now in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Rostock" title="University of Rostock">University of Rostock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-arrow_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arrow-141">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stork_moral_education.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Stork_moral_education.jpg/170px-Stork_moral_education.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Stork_moral_education.jpg/255px-Stork_moral_education.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Stork_moral_education.jpg/340px-Stork_moral_education.jpg 2x" data-file-width="924" data-file-height="1134" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stork_moral_education.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Supposed filial virtues of the stork<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Storks have little fear of humans if not disturbed, and often nest on buildings in Europe. In Germany, the presence of a nest on a house was believed to protect against fires. They were also protected because of the belief that their souls were human.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> German and Dutch households would encourage storks to nest on houses, sometimes by constructing purpose-built high platforms, to bring good luck.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Poles, Lithuanians, Ukrainians and Belarusians believe that storks bring harmony to a family on whose property they nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopaedia_of_Ukraine_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopaedia_of_Ukraine-144">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The white stork is a popular <a href="/wiki/Motif_(visual_arts)" title="Motif (visual arts)">motif</a> on <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamp" title="Postage stamp">postage stamps</a>, and it is featured on more than 120 stamps issued by more than 60 stamp-issuing entities.<sup id="cite_ref-Birdtheme_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birdtheme-145">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_birds" title="List of national birds">national bird</a> of Lithuania, Belarus<sup id="cite_ref-Lithuania_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lithuania-146">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> and Poland, and it was a Polish mascot at the <a href="/wiki/Expo_2000" title="Expo 2000">Expo 2000</a> Fair in Hanover.<sup id="cite_ref-paiiz_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paiiz-147">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Storks nesting in Polish villages such as <a href="/wiki/%C5%BBywkowo,_Podlaskie_Voivodeship" title="Żywkowo, Podlaskie Voivodeship">Zywkowo</a> have made them tourist attractions, drawing 2000–5000 visitors a year in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In the 19th century, storks were also thought to only live in countries having a republican form of government.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Polish poet <a href="/wiki/Cyprian_Kamil_Norwid" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyprian Kamil Norwid">Cyprian Kamil Norwid</a> mentioned storks in his poem <i>Moja piosnka (II)</i> ("My Song (II)"):<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r960796168">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>For the land where it's a great travesty<br />
To harm a stork's nest in a pear tree,<br />
For storks serve us all&#160;...<br />
I am homesick, Lord!&#160;...
</p>
</div><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>translated by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Whipple" title="Walter Whipple">Walter Whipple</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In 1942 <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Heinrich Himmler</a> sought to use storks to carry Nazi propaganda leaflets so as to win support from the <a href="/wiki/Boer" title="Boer">Boers</a> in South Africa. The idea for this "Storchbein-Propaganda" plan was a secret that was transmitted by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Schellenberg" title="Walter Schellenberg">Walter Schellenberg</a> to be examined by the German ornithologist <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Sch%C3%BCz" title="Ernst Schüz">Ernst Schüz</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Rossitten_Bird_Observatory" title="Rossitten Bird Observatory">Rossiten bird observatory</a>, who pointed out that the probability of finding marked storks in Africa was less than one percent, requiring a 1000 birds to transmit 10 leaflets successfully. The plan was then dropped.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storks_and_delivery_of_babies">Storks and delivery of babies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Storks and delivery of babies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg/120px-Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="423" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg/180px-Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg/240px-Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg 2x" data-file-width="702" data-file-height="2476" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carl_Spitzweg_029.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Der Klapperstorch</i> (<i>The Stork</i>), a painting by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Spitzweg" title="Carl Spitzweg">Carl Spitzweg</a> (1808–1885)</div></div></div>
<p>According to European folklore, the stork is responsible for bringing babies to new parents. The legend is very ancient, but was popularised by a 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_Andersen" title="Hans Christian Andersen">Hans Christian Andersen</a> story called "The Storks".<sup id="cite_ref-sax_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sax-140">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> German folklore held that storks found babies in caves or marshes and brought them to households in a basket on their backs or held in their beaks. These caves contained <i>adebarsteine</i> or "stork stones". The babies would then be given to the mother or dropped down the chimney. Households would notify when they wanted children by placing sweets for the stork on the window sill.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> From there the folklore has spread around the world to the Philippines and countries in South America.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_135-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Birthmarks on the back of the head of newborn baby, <i><a href="/wiki/Nevus_flammeus_nuchae" title="Nevus flammeus nuchae">nevus flammeus nuchae</a></i>, are sometimes referred to as stork-bite.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stork_bringing_baby_-_Colmar,_Alsace,_2016.05.18_(35).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Stork_bringing_baby_-_Colmar%2C_Alsace%2C_2016.05.18_%2835%29.jpg/220px-Stork_bringing_baby_-_Colmar%2C_Alsace%2C_2016.05.18_%2835%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Stork_bringing_baby_-_Colmar%2C_Alsace%2C_2016.05.18_%2835%29.jpg/330px-Stork_bringing_baby_-_Colmar%2C_Alsace%2C_2016.05.18_%2835%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Stork_bringing_baby_-_Colmar%2C_Alsace%2C_2016.05.18_%2835%29.jpg/440px-Stork_bringing_baby_-_Colmar%2C_Alsace%2C_2016.05.18_%2835%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stork_bringing_baby_-_Colmar,_Alsace,_2016.05.18_(35).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stork bringing baby - Colmar, Alsace</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Slavic_mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic mythology">Slavic mythology and religion</a>, storks were thought to carry unborn <a href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Soul (spirit)">souls</a> from <a href="/wiki/Vyraj" title="Vyraj">Vyraj</a> to Earth in spring and summer.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> This belief still persists in the modern folk culture of many Slavic countries, in the simplified child story that "storks bring children into the world".<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Storks were seen by <a href="/wiki/Early_Slavs" title="Early Slavs">Early Slavs</a> as bringing luck, and killing one would bring misfortune.<sup id="cite_ref-autonazwa1_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autonazwa1-155">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> A long-term study that showed a <a href="/wiki/Spurious_correlation" class="mw-redirect" title="Spurious correlation">spurious correlation</a> between the numbers of stork nests and human births is widely used in the teaching of basic <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a> as an example to highlight that <a href="/wiki/Correlation_and_dependence" title="Correlation and dependence">correlation</a> does not necessarily indicate <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> The child-bringing myth has appeared in different forms in history. Children of <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African American</a> <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_US" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavery in the US">slaves</a> were sometimes told that white babies were brought by storks, while black babies were born from <a href="/wiki/Buzzard" title="Buzzard">buzzard</a> eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Psychoanalyst Marvin Margolis suggests the enduring nature of the stork fable of the newborn is linked to its addressing a psychological need, in that it allays the discomfort of discussing sex and procreation with children. Birds have long been associated with the maternal symbols from pagan goddesses such as <a href="/wiki/Juno_(mythology)" title="Juno (mythology)">Juno</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Ghost</a>, and the stork may have been chosen for its white plumage (depicting purity), size (in that it is big enough to carry an infant), and flight at high altitude (likened to flying between Earth and Heaven).<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_135-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The fable and its relation to the internal world of the child have been discussed by <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a><sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_135-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jung10_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jung10-159">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> In fact, Jung recalled being told the story himself upon the birth of his own sister.<sup id="cite_ref-Pickles11_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pickles11-160">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> The traditional link with the newborn continues with their use in <a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">advertising</a> for such products as <a href="/wiki/Diaper" title="Diaper">nappies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baby_announcement" title="Baby announcement">baby announcements</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_135-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There were negative aspects to stork folklore as well; a Polish folk tale relates how God made the stork's plumage white, while the Devil gave it black wings, imbuing it with both good and evil impulses. They were also associated with handicapped or stillborn babies in Germany, explained as the stork having dropped the baby en route to the household, or as revenge or punishment for past wrongdoing. A mother who was confined to bed around the time of childbirth was said to have been "bitten" by the stork.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_135-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> In Denmark, storks were said to toss a nestling off the nest and then an egg in successive years.<sup id="cite_ref-Margolis72_135-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margolis72-135">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> In medieval England, storks were also associated with adultery, possibly inspired by their courtship rituals. Their preening and posture saw them linked with the attribute of self-conceit.<sup id="cite_ref-deVries76_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deVries76-161">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=White_stork&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The universally accepted starting point of modern taxonomy for animals is set at 1758, with the publishing of Linnaeus' <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition of <i>Systema Naturae</i></a>, although scientists had been coining names in the previous century.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">By convention, length is measured from the tip of the bill to the tip of the tail on a dead bird (or skin) laid on its back.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFLewisKingery1918" class="citation book cs1">Lewis, Charlton Thomas; Kingery, Hugh Macmaster (1918). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/bub_gb_2ncKAAAAIAAJ"><i>An Elementary Latin Dictionary</i></a>.
